                                                 A BSTRACT
010
011
               Prompt learning has shown to be an efficient and effective fine-tuning method for
012            vision-language models like CLIP. While numerous studies have focused on the
013            generalisation of these models in few-shot classification, their capability in near
014            out-of-distribution (OOD) detection has been overlooked. A few recent works
015            have highlighted the promising performance of prompt learning in far OOD de-
016            tection. However, the more challenging task of few-shot near OOD detection has
017            not yet been addressed. In this study, we investigate the near OOD detection capa-
018            bilities of prompt learning models and observe that commonly used OOD scores
019            have limited performance in near OOD detection. To enhance the performance, we
020
               propose a fast and simple post-hoc method that complements existing logit-based
               scores and can be easily applied to any prompt learning model without change
021
               in architecture or model re-training while keeping the same classification accu-
022
               racy. Our method boosts existing prompt learning methods’ near OOD detection
023            performance in AUROC by up to 11.67% with minimal computational cost. Com-
024            prehensive empirical evaluations across 13 datasets and 8 models demonstrate the
025            effectiveness and adaptability of our method1 .
026
027
028   1   I NTRODUCTION
029
030   Pre-trained vision-language models such as ALIGN (Jia et al., 2021) and CLIP (Radford et al., 2021)
031   have shown outstanding visual-text understanding by learning to align image features and textual
032   features of a large-scale image-text dataset via contrastive learning. Consequently, CLIP naturally
033   excels at zero-shot image classification, utilising a class name as natural language text instead of
034   arbitrarily numbered category. For instance, cosine similarity between encoded image feature of an
035
      image and encoded textual feature of “a photo of a [CLASS].” with a class name as “[CLASS]” can
      perform zero-shot classification without requiring any additional classification head. Logits with
036
      different class names can then be transformed to probabilities by using the softmax function.
037
038   While CLIP’s zero-shot capabilities are impressive, recent studies have highlighted its sensitivity
039   to prompt wording. For instance, Zhou et al. (2022b) demonstrated that small variations in prompt
040   structure (e.g., “a photo of a [CLASS]” vs. “a photo of [CLASS]”) can lead to significant accuracy
041
      drops, sometimes exceeding 5% on standard benchmarks like Caltech101 (Fei-Fei et al., 2004). This
      observation has led to an emerging research direction of prompt learning for few-shot classification
042
      with vision-language models (Zhou et al., 2022b;a; Yao et al., 2023; Zhu et al., 2023; Khattak et al.,
043
      2023a;b), which optimises continuous context vectors in the word-embedding space, eliminating the
044   need for handcrafting prompts.
045
046   Although existing methods have shown success in this area, the majority focus primarily on en-
047
      hancing classification accuracy, leaving the equally important task of out-of-distribution (OOD)
      detection underexplored. OOD detection is crucial for real-world, safety-critical applications such
048
      as autonomous driving, healthcare, and industrial automation, where models must perform reliably
049
      under unfamiliar or unexpected conditions. In these applications, excelling in in-distribution (ID)
050   classification is not enough. Models must also be capable of detecting and effectively handling OOD
051   samples. Here, we focus on the more challenging task of near OOD detection (Yang et al., 2023;
052
        1
053       Codes    are     available               at       https://anonymous.4open.science/r/
      near-OOD-prompt-learning-25D1


                                                        1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      2022; Zhang et al., 2023b; Fort et al., 2021; Ren et al., 2021; Winkens et al., 2020). Near OOD
055   detection refers to a scenario where OOD samples share the same domain as ID samples but have
056   different label space (e.g., both ID and near OOD samples are flower images with no overlapping
057   classes).
058
      In this paper, we focus on developing a post-hoc method to enhance near OOD detection perfor-
059
      mance for prompt learning methods in vision-language models without the need to retrain them. To
060
      the best of our knowledge, only a few existing works address related problems, and none of them
061   address this specific problem directly. Specifically, Bai et al. (2023); Miyai et al. (2023) investi-
062   gated far OOD detection, where OOD samples come from completely different domains. However,
063   these methods are not post-hocs, which require to train the models from scratch. In addition, near
064   OOD tasks are out of their consideration. Existing logit-based OOD scores, such as MaxLogit
065   score (Hendrycks et al., 2022) or Energy score (Liu et al., 2020), which estimate a model’s confi-
066   dence in its predictions, can be applied to our case in a post-hoc manner. However, these scores
067   are not specifically designed for vision-language models like CLIP where overlapping score distri-
068   butions between ID and OOD samples often result in poor detection performance, especially in the
069
      near OOD tasks.
070   We address the above problem by proposing a
071   simple yet novel and effective approach, which Table 1: Improvement of near OOD AUROC us-
072   introduces a new logit-based score named ing MaxLogit score with our method on 1-shot
073   Marginal Logit Score (MLS), computed from Caltech101 (Fei-Fei et al., 2004) with wall-clock
074   the output logits of an existing method. Tai- time measured for the method. Refer to Section 5
075
      lored for vision-language models, the key idea for details.
      of MLS is measuring the difference between

      mentary score named Context score. MLS cre-           CoOp (Zhou et al., 2022b)       +2.66       0.988
078   ates clearer separation between ID and near          CoCoOp (Zhou et al., 2022a)      +5.05       1.703
                                                           IVLP (Khattak et al., 2023a)     +3.79       1.055
079   OOD samples, leading to substantial perfor-           KgCoOp  (Yao et al., 2023)      +7.09       0.989
                                                            ProGrad (Zhu et al., 2023)      +5.90       0.994
080   mance gains. Notably, our method does not re-       MaPLe (Khattak et al., 2023a)     +5.74       0.974
081   quire any change to the model architecture and    PromptSRC (Khattak et al., 2023b)   +6.09       1.005
                                                           LoCoOp (Miyai et al., 2023)     +10.57       1.153
082   does not involve retraining, making it highly ef-
083   ficient and adaptable. Table 1 demonstrates the
084   performance gain of MLS when used with existing methods.
085   We validate our method across 13 diverse datasets and 8 state-of-the-art prompt learning models.
086   Our experiments show that our framework improves near OOD detection performance by up to
087   11.67% in terms of AUROC, without affecting the classification accuracy of the underlying models.
088   This demonstrates the versatility and effectiveness of our approach in real-world applications.
089
090   2     BACKGROUND
091
092   2.1   C ONTRASTIVE L ANGUAGE –I MAGE P RE - TRAINING (CLIP)
093
094   Contrastive Language–Image Pre-training (CLIP) (Radford et al., 2021), a pre-trained vision-
095   language model learned to align 400 million image-text pairs, is renowned for its powerful zero-shot
096   image classification performance. It measures cosine similarity between image feature of an unseen
097   image and textual feature of a text prompt formatted as “a photo of a [CLASS]” where [CLASS] is
098
      the name of a class in a label space of interest. Formally, given an image I ∈ RH×W ×3 with H
      being the height and W being the width and a text prompt T = “a photo of a [CLASS]”, a classifi-
099
      cation logit is computed by ⟨EncI (I), EncT (T )⟩ where ⟨·, ·⟩ is cosine similarity, EncI (·) is an image
100
      encoder, and EncT (·) is a text encoder. The image encoder can be either ResNet (He et al., 2016) or
101   Vision Transformer (ViT) (Dosovitskiy et al., 2021), and the text encoder is Transformer (Vaswani
102   et al., 2017). For the brevity of notation, we omit the notations of the encoders for the remainder of
103   the paper.
104
105   2.2   P ROMPT L EARNING OF CLIP
106
107   Prompt learning of CLIP was first introduced by Zhou et al. (2022b) through Context Optimiza-
      tion (CoOp), which adapts popular prompt learning techniques from the natural language pro-


                                                             2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      cessing (NLP) field to CLIP. It addresses the issue of CLIP’s classification being sensitive to
109   the prompt’s prefix (e.g., a large performance gap between when using “a photo of a [CLASS]”
110   and “a [CLASS]”) by optimising the prefix with few-shot samples. CoOp learns M continuous
111   context vectors V = {V1 , V2 , · · · , VM } within word-embedding space where Vi ∈ RD is the
112   ith vector with D being the word-embedding dimension. The learnable prompt is formalised as
113   P = {V1 , V2 , · · · , VM , C} where C ∈ RD is the word-embedding of a class name appended to
114   the context vectors. The classification logit of ith class is then computed by ⟨I, Pi ⟩ where Pi is the
115   learnable prompt with the ith class name. The probability is estimated by the softmax function as
                                  (⟨I,Pi ⟩/τ )
116   p(y = i|I, Pi ) = PKexpexp     (⟨I,Pk ⟩/τ )
                                                  where K is the total number of classes and τ is the temper-
                              k=1
117   ature scale. Cross-entropy loss is then minimised to learn the context vectors. Note that the only
118   learnable parameters that are common among different prompt learning models are the M context
119   vectors. Since the introduction of CoOp, a number of subsequent works have aimed to improve its
120   ID accuracy and generalisability with modifications in model architecture or additional loss terms
121   (See Section 4). The aim of the paper is to develop a post-hoc approach that improves near OOD
122   detection performance while being agnostic to base prompt learning models.
123
124   2.3   N EAR O UT-O F -D ISTRIBUTION D ETECTION
125
126
      OOD detection is largely categorised as far OOD detection and near OOD detection based on the dis-
      tribution shift between an ID test dataset and an OOD dataset along with difficulty of detection (Ren
127
      et al., 2021; Fort et al., 2021; Yang et al., 2021; 2022; Zhang et al., 2023b). Far OOD datasets have
128
      covariate shift in images (i.e., OOD samples are from domains that differ from the training set), and
129   near OOD datasets which are more challenging to detect involve semantic shift (i.e., OOD samples
130   are drawn from the same domain as the training set but belong to previously unseen label classes).
131   Near OOD detection is also synonymous with fine-grained OOD detection (Zhang et al., 2023a) and
132   hard OOD detection (Li et al., 2024; Ming et al., 2022).
133
      In this paper, we study near OOD detection via prompt learning of CLIP, a new research problem
134
      to which no existing methods are tailored. Given a trained CLIP prompt learning method, we focus
135   on post-hoc approaches that compute a score from the logits of the method to determine whether a
136   given image is from ID or from OOD, which can be written as:
137
                                                      1 S I; {Pi }K

138                                       K                         i=1   ≥ α

                                g I; {Pi }i=1 , α =                                                (1)
139                                                   0 S I; {Pi }K i=1 < α
140
141
      where g(·) is a OOD detector, α is the threshold, and S(·) is a score function. By convention, the
      ground truth label is 1 for ID samples and 0 for OOD samples.
142
143
144   3     M ETHOD
145
146   3.1   P ROBLEM S ETTING
147
148   We focus on a near OOD detection problem for prompt learning models of CLIP, which is to detect
                                                                   ID       ID
149   whether a given image Itest is from the ID test dataset Dtest    of (Itest , y ID ) pairs or a near OOD
                nearOOD        nearOOD nearOOD                       ID
150
      dataset Dtest       of (Itest      ,y          ) pairs where y    ∈ {1, · · · , K} is the ID label with
      K classes and y nearOOD ∈ {1, · · · , L} is the near OOD label with L classes. The ID dataset and
151
      the near OOD dataset contain the same types of images (i.e., no covariate shift in images) but have
152
      no overlapping classes (i.e., y ID ∩ y nearOOD = ∅). Without loss of generality, we assume that the
153   context vectors V have already been fine-tuned using a prompt learning model with a few-shot ID
154   training dataset and only consider post-training stage in a post-hoc manner.
155
156
      3.2   M OTIVATION
157
158   Energy and MaxLogit scores are two widely-used scores in the OOD detection literature (Liu et al.,
159   2020; Hendrycks et al., 2022; Yang et al., 2021; Sun et al., 2022; Yang et al., 2022; Zhang et al.,
160   2023b; Han et al., 2022; Sun et al., 2021). In our prompt learning context, these scores can be used
161   to measure a model’s confidence from the output logits (i.e., cosine similarity between image and
      textual features), based on the assumption that ID samples typically have higher scores than OOD

178   samples. Specifically, we have:

187   Although these logit-based scores have been commonly used for OOD detection, they perform sub-
188   optimally in near OOD tasks. This is because near OOD samples often generate logit distributions
189   that closely resemble those of ID samples, causing significant overlap between their score distri-
190   butions. Figure 1 demonstrates this with a real-world dataset in the near OOD setting, where the
191   overlap makes it difficult to distinguish between the two, leading to poor detection performance.
192
193   3.3     P ROPOSED S CORE F UNCTION
194
195   Our objective is to develop a post-hoc method that improves MaxLogit and Energy scores to enhance
196
      near OOD detection for any CLIP-based prompt learning model with minimal learning cost.
197
      3.3.1    C ONTEXT S CORE AND M ARGINAL L OGIT S CORE
198
199   We first introduce the concept of Context Score SContext = ⟨I, V ⟩, which represents the cosine
200   similarity between the image feature and the textual feature from the context vectors without any
201   class name. The intuition is that the context vectors capture the general features of the model without
202   associating images with any specific class and this score reflects how well the image aligns with
203   these generic and non-class-specific features. The key insight is that for ID samples, the model
204   should have a strong class-specific association, and therefore the Context score should be relatively
205   low. Conversely, for OOD samples, the model’s uncertainty should result in a higher Context score.
206
      Given a prompt learning method, Context score can be easily obtained by adding an additional label
      class with no class name (i.e., extending K classes to K + 1 classes).
207
208   We argue that Context score is a valuable complement to MaxLogit or Energy score, especially
209   when the difference between these scores is leveraged. Ideally, when a model is given an ID image,
210   it confidently predicts that the image belongs to a specific ID class, resulting in high Energy or
211   MaxLogit score which represents the model’s confidence. In contrast, its Context score is much
212
      lower, as it is calculated using a prompt lacking the ground-truth class name. This creates a large
      gap between the original score and the Context score.
213
214   Conversely, when the model is given a near OOD image, MaxLogit or Energy score is small as the
215   model is uncertain about the image belonging to any ID class. As a result, the gap between the
      original score and Context score is much smaller. This difference between Energy or MaxLogit

226   Flowers102 (Nilsback & Zisserman, 2008) with the MaxLogit score shown in blue and the Context

228   not distinguishable (left bar plot), MLS which subtracts Context score from MaxLogit score is much
229   more distinguishable (right bar plot).
230
231
      score and Context score can be viewed as an alternative score, which we name Marginal Logit Score
232
      (MLS) defined as:
233
234                       Marginal Logit Score (Energy) SMLS-E = SEnergy − SContext                              (4)
235                       Marginal Logit Score (MaxLogit) SMLS-M = SMaxLogit − SContext                          (5)
236
237   To demonstrate the effectiveness of MLS, we present an ID image and a near OOD image from
238   Flowers102 (Nilsback & Zisserman, 2008) in Figure 2, with MaxLogit scores highlighted in blue
239   and Context scores in grey. The MaxLogit score for the OOD sample is higher than that for the ID
240
      sample, indicating the model’s failure to differentiate between the two. However, when the Context
      score is subtracted from the MaxLogit score, MLS is higher for the ID sample, showing that the
241
      model can successfully distinguish between the two samples.
242
243   We further illustrate its geometric interpretation in Figure 3a and Figure 3b where MaxLogit score
244   and MLS-M are plotted at y-axis and Context score is plotted at x-axis. Geometrically, subtracting
245   Context score from MaxLogit score is the same as applying vertical shearing transformation (Lax,
246
      2007) to the samples, which essentially reduces the overlapping area highlighted by shaded boxes.
      With the MaxLogit score, the near OOD AUROC is 0.785 which is increased to 0.879 when evalu-
247
      ated with MLS-M.
248
249
      3.3.2      M ARGIN S CALE
250
251   While the simple subtraction of the Context score improves OOD performance, its fixed margin
252   limits adaptability across different models and datasets. To address this, we introduce a coefficient
253   β termed margin scale that controls the amount of reduction of Context score from MaxLogit or
254   Energy score in a more flexible manner:
255                      Marginal Logit Score (Energy) SMLS-E = SEnergy − β · SContext                           (6)
256
                         Marginal Logit Score (MaxLogit) SMLS-M = SMaxLogit − β · SContext                       (7)
257
258   In Figure 3c, we show that if the margin scale is applied, the near OOD AUROC is improved from
259   0.879 (equivalent to β = 1) to 0.942 (β = 2.2). To further demonstrate the importance of β to
260   the base prompt learning models, we show how the near OOD performance in AUROC of different
261   models varies with different β in Figure 4, where β = 0 represents the case of the original score
262   without the context score and β = 1 is MLS without the margin scale. One can see that the value of
263   β significantly affects the performance with all the prompt learning models.
264   This naturally opens a question on how to set β properly. If we were given the OOD samples, we
265   could simply choose the value of β that minimises the near OOD performance. However, such
266   an approach is impractical for real-world applications where near OOD samples are unavailable
267   before deployment. To address this, we propose to estimate the margin scale by only using few-
268   shot ID training samples. Initially, MaxLogit score and Context score in Figure 3a exhibit positive
269   correlation, leading to significant overlap in the density distributions of ID and near OOD samples.
      When the near OOD detection AUROC is maximised, as shown in Figure 3c, this correlation is


                                                                                   5
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

279            (a) SMaxLogit               (b) SMLS-M (β = 1)             (c) SMLS-M (β = 2.2)
280
      Figure 3: (a) MaxLogit score, (b) MLS-M without β, and (c) MLS-M with β of test ID and near
281
      OOD samples with respect to Context scores. Areas where ID samples and near OOD samples
282   overlap are highlighted with shaded boxes. All scores are computed using MaPLe (Khattak et al.,
283   2023a) on Caltech101 (Fei-Fei et al., 2004). See Appendix A.4 for additional demonstrations with
284   different models and datasets.
285
286
287   minimised, resulting in reduced overlap between the distributions. Thus, we formulate this problem
288   as finding the margin scale that minimises the correlation between MaxLogit or Energy score and
289   Context score.
290   We propose approximating this correlation using the covariance matrix of a bivariate normal distri-
291   bution fitted with MLS and Context scores computed from the training samples. A key advantage
292   of this approach is its computational simplicity and the availability of a closed-form solution via
293   maximum likelihood estimation. By leveraging this, we find the margin scale that zeros out the
294   off-diagonals of the covariance matrix of the fitted bivariate normal distribution.
295   Lemma 3.1. Given N scalar observations {x̂i }N                   N
                                                        i=1 and {ŷi }i=1 , we define two variables x = x̂
296   and y = ŷ − β · x̂. The scale parameter β that zeros out the covariance of two variables (i.e., the
297   off-diagonals of a covariance matrix) which is approximated by maximum likelihood estimation is:
298                                          PN
                                                   (x̂i − µx̂ )(ŷi − µŷ )
299                                     β = i=1  PN                                                    (8)
                                                                    2
300                                                 i=1 (x̂i − µx̂ )
301                   PN                    PN
302   where µx̂ = N1 i=1 x̂i and µŷ = N1 i=1 ŷi .
303
      By using Lemma 3.1 with ŷ being the MaxLogit score or Energy score and x̂ being the Context score,
304
      the margin scale can be easily estimated with ID training samples (see Appendix A.1 for proof of
305
      the lemma). This scale estimation needs to be conducted only once after training finishes. Figure 4
306   shows the estimated margin scale in red dotted lines, demonstrating that our estimation is close to the
307   value that results in the best performance. In addition to good accuracy, our method is a close-form
308   estimation that only takes a small number of ID training samples with little computational cost.
309

      4   R ELATED W ORK
312
      Vision-Language Models Vision-language models have significantly advanced in recent years,
313
      bridging the gap between visual and textual data. Early approaches, such as image captioning mod-
314
      els (Karpathy & Fei-Fei, 2015; Wang et al., 2016; You et al., 2016), typically used convolutional
315   neural networks (CNNs) to extract visual features and recurrent neural networks (RNNs) to generate
316   descriptive text. The advent of transformers (Vaswani et al., 2017) handling long-range dependen-
317   cies more effectively and contrastive learning (Oord et al., 2018) revolutionised this field. Notably,
318   ALIGN (Jia et al., 2021), CLIP (Radford et al., 2021), and LiT (Zhai et al., 2022) leveraged a con-
319   trastive learning framework that aligns image and text embeddings in a multimodal space, allowing
320   for zero-shot learning capabilities and impressive generalisation to unseen tasks and datasets. In this
321   work, we leverage the powerful vision-language model CLIP and extend its near OOD capability.
322
323   CLIP-based Prompt Learning Despite the remarkable zero-shot performance of CLIP, CLIP
      shows inherently unstable classification accuracy that varies by wording of prompt. To mitigate this

341
342
343       issue, CoOp (Zhou et al., 2022b) was proposed to optimise a prompt in word embedding space,
344       leveraging prompt learning from the NLP literature. CoCoOp (Zhou et al., 2022a) identified that
345       CoOp has limited generalisation and proposed to condition image features to the learnable prompt.
346       Subsequently, many studies have proposed different techniques to improve the generalisation (Yao
347
          et al., 2023; Zhu et al., 2023; Khattak et al., 2023a;b). While its generalisation has been largely
          improved, its OOD detection has been overlooked. LoCoOp (Miyai et al., 2023) proposed a OOD
348
          regularisation to improve OOD detection performance. Nevertheless, no study has addressed near
349
          OOD detection of prompt learning models.
350
351
          OOD Detection An early work of Hendrycks & Gimpel (2017) utilised the maximum softmax
352       probability (MSP) as a score to identify OOD samples. Another notable work is Out-of-DIstribution
353       detector for Neural networks (ODIN) (Liang et al., 2018) which extends MSP by introducing tem-
354       perature scaling and input pre-processing to enhance separation of the scores from ID samples and
355       OOD samples. Similar to ODIN, Mahalanobis (Lee et al., 2018) score also uses input pre-processing
356       in addition to measuring distance in feature space. Delving into a more challenging task of near
357       OOD detection, several studies analysed benchmarks of pre-trained networks in near OOD detec-
358       tion (Yang et al., 2023; 2022; Zhang et al., 2023b; Fort et al., 2021), and different training methods
359       and score functions were proposed for near OOD detection (Ren et al., 2021; Winkens et al., 2020).
360
          Despite significant advancements in OOD detection for traditional classifier-based neural networks,
          many existing methods are not directly applicable to CLIP-based prompt learning models, which
361
          lack classifier heads. Furthermore, since these models do not update their image encoders during
362
          fine-tuning, many distance-based methods that rely on image features become ineffective.
363
364
365       5              E XPERIMENTS
366
367       5.1             E XPERIMENTAL S ETTINGS
368
369
          Datasets Following previous works of CLIP-based prompt learning models (Zhou et al., 2022a;
          Khattak et al., 2023a; Yao et al., 2023; Zhu et al., 2023; Khattak et al., 2023a;b; Miyai et al., 2023),
370
          we use 11 publicly available datasets of ImageNet (Deng et al., 2009), Caltech101 (Fei-Fei et al.,
371
          2004), OxfordPets (Parkhi et al., 2012), StanfordCars (Krause et al., 2013), Flowers102 (Nilsback &
372       Zisserman, 2008), Food101 (Bossard et al., 2014), FGVCAircraft (Maji et al., 2013), SUN397 (Xiao
373       et al., 2010), DTD (Cimpoi et al., 2014), EuroSAT (Helber et al., 2019), and UCF101 (Soomro et al.,
374       2012). A common task of these works involves training models on half of the label classes (e.g.,
375       base classes) and evaluating them on the other half classes (e.g., new classes) to measure base-to-
376       new generalisation. We reframe this task as a near OOD detection problem. Specifically, the models
377       trained on base classes are tested with a dataset where half of the samples belong to the base classes
          (ID) and the other half to new classes (near OOD). The task is to detect whether each test image

      Models We use 8 prompt learning models: CoOp (Zhou et al., 2022b), CoCoOp (Zhou et al.,
426   2022a), IVLP (Khattak et al., 2023a), KgCoOp (Yao et al., 2023), ProGrad (Zhu et al., 2023),
427   MaPLe (Khattak et al., 2023a), PromptSRC (Khattak et al., 2023b), and LoCoOp (Miyai et al.,

429   former (Vaswani et al., 2017) for the text encoder. We follow their training details to train them
430   with 16, 8, 4, 2, and 1-shot settings using 3 random seeds (see Appendix A.2 for implementation de-
431   tails). For each model, we use MaxLogit score, Energy score, and MCM (Ming et al., 2022) score as
      baselines where MCM is the state-of-the-art score for CLIP. As our focus is on the post-hoc method

449   5.2   E XPERIMENTAL R ESULTS
450
451   We report average AUROC and false positive rate (FPR) of near OOD samples when true positive
452   rate (TPR) of ID samples is at 95% with 16, 8, 4, 2, and 1-shot settings and 3 randoms seeds.
453   Refer to Appendix A.3 for results of individual few-shot settings with standard deviations across the
454
      random seeds. In Table 2, we compare near OOD detection AUROC using the MaxLogit score and
      MLS-M score. Positive improvements in AUROC are observed in 100 out of 104 evaluations (i.e.,
455
      13 datasets×8 models) when MLS-M is used.
456
457   On average, the largest improvement was observed with LoCoOp, the recent prompt learning model
458   for OOD detection. The same results for Energy score are shown in Table 12. Similar to MaxLogit
459   score, AUROC is improved in 101 out of 104 evaluations when using Energy score. Refer to Table 6
460
      and Table 13 for FPR95.
461   Table 3 shows a comparison between MaxLogit score, Energy score, MLS-M, MLS-E, and MCM,
462   in terms of both AUROC and FPR95 averaged across 13 datasets. The MLS-M and Energy score
463   outperform MCM in both AUROC and FPR95. When MLS-M and MLS-E are compared, MLS-M
464   outperforms MLS-E for the half of the models in AUROC and all models in FPR95. This highlights
465   that the proposed scores are better scores for near OOD detection tasks than MCM, and MLS-M is
466
      better than MLS-E. Refer to Table 21 and Table 22 for the results of all datasets.
467
468   6     D ISCUSSION
469
470   Comparison with MCM As discussed in Section 5.2, MLS outperforms MCM in AUROC and
471   FPR95 averaged across 13 datasets. We notice that MCM outperforms these scores in a few datasets.
472   We analyse this difference from the perspective of dataset distance. As MCM is the state-of-the-art
473   OOD detection score for far OOD detection, MCM is expected to outperform as the dataset distance
474   between an ID test dataset and a near OOD dataset increases. We empirically validate this assump-
475
      tion by measuring the dataset distance by Optimal Transport Dataset Distance (OTDD) (Alvarez-
      Melis & Fusi, 2020) which is shown in Figure 5. For each dataset, we measure the distance between
476
      the ID test dataset and the near OOD test dataset. The density is then plotted, with blue indicating
477
      areas where MLS performs better and grey indicating areas where MCM performs better. While
478   MLS outperforms across a wide range of distance, it excels when the distance decreases. MCM, on
479   the other hand, tends to outperform as the distance increases. Nevertheless, MLS outperforms in the
480   majority of cases, resulting in superior average performance.
481
482   Application to MCM A straightforward extension of our method is the application of the post-hoc
483   process to the MCM score (i.e., SMLS-MCM = SMCM − β · SContext ). The differences in AUROC and
484   FPR95 with MCM and MLS-MCM are presented in Table 19 and Table 20. While it improves per-
485   formance with MCM on most datasets, some datasets exhibit a decline in performance. This decline
      is due to the lack of a positive correlation between MCM and the Context score, which was ob-


                                                         9
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


486
      served with logit-based scores in Figure 3. Figure 6 further demonstrates the differing relationships
487   between the score and the Context score. This discrepancy primarily arises from the softmax nor-
488   malisation, where MCM only considers the relative magnitude of logits between classes, allowing
489   non-maximum logits to unexpectedly change the relationship.
490
491
492
      Application to Far OOD Detection Al-
493
      though our method is not designed for far         0.05

513
514
      Comparisons with More OOD Methods In our experiments, we intentionally excluded OOD
515   methods that require access to OOD samples during training, retraining the prompt learning models,
516   or those incompatible with the fine-tuned CLIP-based prompt learning models. This is because our
517   main contribution is a new score specifically designed for few-shot prompt learning models without
518   these requirements. For example, ODIN (Liang et al., 2018) is not a suitable baseline as it requires
519   access to OOD samples for hyperparameter tuning. Similarly, LogitNorm (Wei et al., 2022) needs
520   to be trained with a specific loss function, requiring the retraining of prompt learning models with
521   modified training objectives. Distance-based scores, such as Mahalanobis distance (Lee et al., 2018)
522   and relative Mahalanobis distance (RMD) (Ren et al., 2021), were originally designed for traditional
523
      classifiers and are therefore incompatible with few-shot prompt learning models. For example, to
      compute RMD in our case, only image features can be used while the textual features are ignored.
524
      As the prompt learning models do not update their image encoders while fine-tuning, RMD will
525
      be the same regardless of prompt learning model used. Therefore, these scores are not suitable for
526   our problem. However, we provide comparisons with LogitNorm and RMD in Appendix A.3.8 for
527   readers interested in the comparisons.
528
529
530   7   C ONCLUSION
531
532
      In this work, we address few-shot near OOD detection of CLIP-based prompt learning models. To
533
      enhance existing logit-based scores, we propose a simple and fast post-hoc method applicable to
534   any prompt learning model. Without changing training procedures of the existing models nor com-
535   promising classification accuracy, our method effectively enhances near OOD AUROC and lowers
536   FPR95 for 8 recent prompt learning models across 13 real-world datasets. While our method is
537   broadly applicable across various prompt learning models, the degree of improvement can vary de-
538   pending on the underlying model characteristics. Some models may not see as substantial a benefit,
539   particularly if their inherent logit distributions already exhibit strong separability between ID and
      OOD samples.

        A.1    P ROOF OF L EMMA
759
760     We provide the proof of Lemma 3.1. For completeness of proof, we duplicate the lemma here.
761
        Lemma A.1. Given N scalar observations {x̂i }N                   N
                                                          i=1 and {ŷi }i=1 , we define two variables x = x̂
762
        and y = ŷ − β · x̂. The scale parameter β that zeros out the covariance of two variables (i.e., the
763     off-diagonals of a covariance matrix) which is approximated by maximum likelihood estimation is:
764
                                               PN
765                                                  (x̂i − µx̂ )(ŷi − µŷ )
                                          β = i=1  PN                                                    (9)
766                                                                   2
                                                      i=1 (x̂i − µx̂ )
767
                        PN                    PN
768     where µx̂ = N1 i=1 x̂i and µŷ = N1 i=1 ŷi .
769
770     Proof. It is well known that maximum likelihood estimation (MLE) of bivariate normal distribution
771     for N observations of variables x and y results in (Bishop, 2013):
772
773
                                 1 X                1 X
                           µx =       xi , µy =            yi                                        (10)
774                             N i                N i
775                                                                      T
                                 σ    σxy      1 X xi            µ         xi   µ
776                        Σ = xx           =                 − x             − x                    (11)
                                 σxy σyy       N i        yi     µy        yi   µy
777
778                                1 X
                           σxy =        (xi − µx )(yi − µy )                                         (12)
779                               N i
780
781     where µx and µy are the means of x and y, and Σ is the covariance matrix. We let x = x̂ and
782     y = ŷ − β · x̂ and find β that makes σxy = 0. By rewriting σxy in terms of x̂ and ŷ, we obtain β as:
783                                1 X
784                            σxy =     (x̂i − µx̂ )(ŷi − β · x̂i − µŷ + β · µx̂ ) = 0                       (13)
                                   N i
785                                P
786                                  (x̂i − µx̂ )(ŷi − µŷ )
                                 β= iP                  2
                                                                                                                (14)
787                                      i (x̂i − µx̂ )
788
        The resulting β is the ratio of covariance of x̂ and ŷ to variance of x̂.
789
790
791     A.2    I MPLEMENTATION D ETAILS
792
        We follow the officially released training guidelines for each prompt learning model
793
        using the same configuration files.               The only additional line of code required is
794     beta=(((y-y.mean())*(x-x.mean())).sum())/(((x-x.mean())**2).sum())
795     to estimate the margin scale in Eq.(8). The overall algorithm is summarised in Algorithm 1. Table 4
796     shows common hyperparameters which are the number of epochs, batch size, and context vectors
797     initialisation. Refer to their officially released codes for other model-specific hyperparameters. All
798     models were trained on a single NVIDIA GeForce RTX 3090 GPU with PyTorch framework. The
799     temperature scaling is 0.01 for the Energy score and 1 for the MCM score.
800
801     Algorithm 1: MLS Computation
802     Input: Few-shot training dataset of N image-label pairs of {Ii , yi }N  i=1 where yi ∈ {1, · · · , K}
803               with K classes, a test image Itest , a fine-tuned prompt learning model with learned
804               context vectors V .
      1 for Ii , yi do

806
      2     Compute and store MaxLogit score SMaxLogit or Energy score SEnergy by Eq.(3) and Eq.(2).
      3     Compute and store Context score SContext .
807
      4 end for
808
      5 Estimate margin scale β by Eq.(8)
809
      6 Compute MLS by Eq.(7) and Eq.(6)

       A.3.6   C OMPARISON WITH Z ERO -S HOT CLIP OOD D ETECTION
1945
1946   Although our work is specifically designed for few-shot prompt learning CLIP models and is
1947   not comparable to zero-shot CLIP OOD detection models, we here provide a comparison with
1948   CLIPN (Wang et al., 2023) in Table 25. Following the original setting, we use the maximum softmax
1949   probability as an OOD score. Note that MLS cannot be applied to CLIPN as it is a zero-shot model
1950   which lacks the context vectors.

1987   A.3.8   OTHER OOD S CORES
1988
1989
       We intentionally excluded OOD scores that require model retraining, architectural modifications,
       access to OOD samples, or those that are incompatible with fine-tuned prompt learning models. For
1990
       examples, LogitNorm (Wei et al., 2022) requires training a model with its dedicated training loss to
1991
       use the score, and relative Mahalanobis distance (RMD) (Ren et al., 2021) is intended to be used
1992   with a traditional classifier which has a classifier head. Even if RMD is applied to image features of
1993   the CLIP prompt learning models, all prompt learning models output the same RMD score as their
1994   image networks are not optimised during fine-tuning. Nonetheless, we provide experimental results
1995   of LogitNorm and RMD in Table 27, using 12 datasets excluding ImageNet with 16, 8, 4, and 2-shot
1996   settings. For LogitNorm, we trained the prompt learning models with LogitNorm loss substituting
1997   the original cross entropy loss. Following the original setting in (Wei et al., 2022), we used the
       temperature scale of 0.04 and calculated the maximum softmax probability score.
